0451196716|4.0|About a month ago, I picked up my three-fourths unfinished copy of Stephen King's The Bachman Books. I say three-fourths because I had only read the first of four short novels contained in the book, the now out-of-print Rage, several years ago now. Rage is a dark and un-fantastic story that I didn't care for much, and the fact that it is now out of print is a good thing (King has admitted this himself). Despite this rare miss for King, other avid fans consider these four novels to be among his best work. Regarding the other three books in the collection, I would say my opinion falls somewhere in the middle of the road; these are not his very best works, but they are also far from his worst.<br />I'm going to skip over Rage altogether for the aforementioned reason; it simply isn't that good and there isn't a whole lot to say about it. The true King fan ought to read it for sake of inclusion, and there is some classic-King dialogue in it that warrants a nod, but plot-wise it is territory that most folks wouldn't enjoy dwelling upon.<br />The Long Walk, deals with a futuristic game-show of sorts in which all the contestants but the winner end up dead (it shares similarities with the last story in the Bachman collection, The Running Man). This story jumps right in with little backstory; a teen boy has been selected for this deadly annual competition called The Long Walk and his mother is driving him to the starting point. It involves exactly one hundred contestants, all male, all in their teens or young twenties, and the objective is to walk without stopping for as far as you can until you fall over dead. Slow down, stop, or violate the rules in any other way: you get a warning. Three warnings in a row, and you 'buy a ticket,' which means to be shot by one of the supervising soldiers on the sidelines. The prize: lifelong fame and riches for your entire family (starting to sound a little like The Hunger Games, is it not?). The participants volunteer for the game, which is televised, and it is somewhat of an honor to be accepted. The game is sponsored by a shadowy government and headed up by an equally mysterious official called 'The Colonel.'<br />Initially, I was very intrigued by the idea but quickly began to wonder how King could possibly turn this idea into a 200-something page novel; it sounded more like a short story to me. But he does an excellent job of keeping things interesting, both with the initial shock of boys being killed early on in the competition and with lengthy, yet engaging, dialogue between the boys regarding their pasts, their connections to the Long Walk, their plans for spending if they win, and other entertaining (and often crude) teenage-boy banter.<br />As the story unfolds, you find yourself rooting more and more for a handful of the competing boys (or aching for some of the troublesome ones to fall out) and all the while knowing that all but one of them will surely die. There is a claustrophobic, choking element to the story as you move along the road with them, realizing more personally what a sickening experience this would be, with literally no way out once you've begun but to win, or be shot. King uses a wide array of adjectives to convey just how tired these young men are becoming as they walk through one, then two, then three entire days and nights with not a single stop to rest, and maintaining the required speed of at least four miles per hour. Their feet aching is only the beginning; there's going to the bathroom to consider, charley horses in the legs to avoid, cramps from drinking too much water, salvaging the food they carry on their person, not falling asleep as they walk through the dark, and all the while not just giving up hope and sitting down on the pavement in defeat. There were parts of this book that just made me physically weary, perhaps like reading a Jack London novel can make one imagine they feel very cold.<br />The ending leaves a little bit to be desired as it stops abruptlyand partially open-ended. Just the same, it is perhaps the right ending, as the winning character finds himself in a bit of a catch-22 in the presence of his oppressive government, and it allows the reader to formulate in his own imagination what might have happened.<br />Overall, it is a vivid and uncomfortable novel in the most compelling of ways, and is ultimately worth the journey.and partially open-ended. Just the same, it is perhaps the right ending, as the winning character finds himself in a bit of a catch-22 in the presence of his oppressive government, and it allows the reader to formulate in his own imagination what might have happened.<br />Overall, it is a vivid and uncomfortable novel in the most compelling of ways, and is ultimately worth the journey.

0451197968|4.0|The Running Man is perhaps the best story in Stephen King's Bachman collection. It is fast-paced and original, and curiously enough King states in the introduction that he wrote it start to finish in a 72-hour mad-dash flurry of typing and made very few changes to the final draft.<br /><br />Similarly to The Long Walk, it is about a deadly game show, and takes place in a version of the future where all television is regulated by the government, and all that is on are deadly game shows, as humans have come to a point where other people suffering is the only thing they find entertaining. Most of the world lives in extreme poverty, amongst trash- and drug-laden streets where prostitution is a common and necessary occupation for young mothers. Everyone is forced to own a television and everyone is forced to keep it on all the time, never free from the presence of the fatal game shows. People volunteer to appear on the shows often because they have no other choice to make money.<br /><br />One such volunteer is Ben Richards, a young man too poor to buy medicine for his terminally ill daughter. He begrudgingly goes through the rigorous physical and mental screening for the shows, ends up scoring quite high, and is accepted onto the ultimate game, The Running Man, where the risk is the highest but the winnings the greatest. The survival rate on Running Man is zero. The singular contestant is given a twelve-hour headstart to flee as far and fast as he can, using whatever methods necessary, to avoid the ominous Hunters; men who are sent out by the show's producers with the sole purpose of tracking down the contestant and killing him.<br /><br />More is unraveled about the corrupt, Big-Brother-esque government and the condition of the futuristic country that has become a wasteland as the book goes on. Civilians are offered a $100.00 prize for providing accurate information about the contestant's location; $1000.00 if the info leads to a kill. In a way, the entire country is against Ben Richards as he runs, and the masses watch in lusty anticipation of his violent, on-screen death.<br /><br />This story is just wild, and has some pretty cool twists and turns as Richards writhes his way across the country in evasion of the Hunters, a few times escaping some seemingly impossible positions. It is quick and fun and packs a bang, displaying the best of King's ability to keep things thrilling and fresh. The chapters are even marked in reverse order from 100 down to 0 as the clock ticks down for Ben Richards. There are multiple ideas presented in the story that are both original and familiar, perhaps giving a nod to futuristic-utopia novels like 1984 or Brave New World. It ended up being my favorite of the Bachman Books, for it is exciting, unique, and feels very different from any other King work I have read thus far. Plus, the climax is fantastic.<br /><br />There's a movie adaptation loosely based upon King's book with (of all people) Arnold Schwarzenegger, and I cannot yet attest to the quality of that film (and my expectations are sadly low, considering the time-period in which it was made), but the book is definitely worth a read, not only for King fans, but also for frequenters of sci-fi or thrillers, as The Running Man is a pleasant twist of both.gainst Ben Richards as he runs, and the masses watch in lusty anticipation of his violent, on-screen death.<br /><br />This story is just wild, and has some pretty cool twists and turns as Richards writhes his way across the country in evasion of the Hunters, a few times escaping some seemingly impossible positions. The chapters are even marked in reverse order from 100 down to 0 as the clock ticks down for Ben Richards. There are multiple ideas presented in the story that are both original and familiar, perhaps giving a nod to futuristic-utopia novels like 1984 or Brave New World. Plus, the climax is fantastic.<br /><br />There's a movie adaptation loosely based upon King's book with (of all people) Arnold Schwarzenegger, and I cannot yet attest to the quality of that film (and my expectations are sadly low, considering the time-period in which it was made), but the book is definitely worth a read, not only for King fans, but also for frequenters of sci-fi or thrillers, as The Running Man is a pleasant twist of both.

0451197879|3.0|Roadwork is perhaps the oddest in the Bachman collection, as it is somewhat inconsistent in that it begins one way and ends in quite another. I may very well have just interpreted it wrongly, or it may just be an example of the 'mistakes' of a young writer.<br /><br />It has to do with a man, Bart, who has just received notice that the building of a freeway overpass is going to push him out of his home and his job, and to say the least: he isn't happy about it. The opening pages find him buying a high-powered rifle and conversing silently with two zany personalities, Fred and George, inside his head. Bart is offered a reasonable sum for his home, and is also given opportunity to relocate the laundromat at which he has worked for many years and in which he has secured a semi-high position. Instead, in a fury of anger at the injustice being done against him and his wife, he takes matters into his own hands and sets out to sabotage the freeway construction.<br /><br />While the book begins with an intriguing level of, well, craziness coming from the main character (you get the feeling that Bart has more than a few screws loose), as it carries on things get somewhat more tame and unexciting. Bart's obsession with revenge against the roadwork company ultimately ends his marriage. He has an affair with a teen hitchhiker, but the inclusion of this section feels like page-filler as it is completely unnecessary. His pursuits of vengeance take him to a mafia member's office in search of explosives and to a construction site with a car-full of molotov cocktails, all leading up to a showdown with the police while holed up in his house and armed to the teeth, refusing to allow the bulldozers to level the home. But all of these events come and go with little significance, and the action is too few and far between in a book with such a premise as this. It is primarily plot-driven with little else to cling to.<br /><br />What bothered me most of all was that the two crazy personalities in Bart's head, Fred and George, are all but forgotten by the end. King makes frequent reference to them in the beginning, but by the end (when one would think a crazy person was only getting crazier and talking to himself more) they are scarcely mentioned. It's like they are an element that was included for a purpose that the author forgot to carry through.<br /><br />It was harder to stick with this one simply because it wasn't all that exciting. It has it's moments of excitement or humor as most of King's works do, but it tends to dwell in places that are honestly a bit boring and has an overall lack of direction. The climax is decent but expected, and can be seen coming from many pages away. I don't mean to sound overly harsh on Roadwork, and I certainly feel that all of King's books warrant a look for one reason or another. This one is not all bad; it just isn't great. Others may find more between the pages than I did.ties in Bart's head, Fred and George, are all but forgotten by the end. 1612191290|1.0|I was assigned this book for an upper-level Econ class based around responses from different angles towards Smith's Wealth of Nations and capitalism.  I am conservative in terms of economics but have had my views challenged and even altered by previously assigned books this semester, which range from Marx, Polyani, and Veblen to modern works by Hacker and Pierson.  I believe that I have taken these all with an open mind, and was expecting some interesting information presented here by Graeber.  Instead, I often found myself having to go back and reread entire sections in order to even figure out which direction he was going, and even then I rarely could understand what the argument he was trying to make was.  The general thesis was that debt was necessary to some extent (agreed), and while he could have made this argument strictly from an economic basis, he instead extends it to impossible lengths by straying into the moral area.  He argues that some debts should not be enforced because of the conditions on which they are loaned but does not offer any real way to measure where the line should be drawn on this moral issue.  Who is to say which debts are to be paid and which aren't?  Graeber also makes the point that not all debts have to be paid back by pointing to the Bank of England, which was started by a loan from the bankers to the King, which can't be paid back without destroying the base on which the bank is built.  This is true, but was clearly a calculated move and was never supposed to be paid back.<br /><br />Graeber also didn't do a great job of backing up his opinions with any proof, instead using broad statements such as \\"generally recognized\\" fairly liberally throughout the book.  I was disappointed by this book.  I had hoped for some insights into the recent economic downturn and instead found another piece of pop econ that is lacking in research and objectivity.

B006UG90RM|3.0|Not that the world needs another fan review of Van Halen's new record, but I just gotta. So hear it is, a completely biased and subjective break down of \\"A Different Kind of Truth\\" from a Van Halen apologist's point of view.<br /><br />I'll start with Wolfie, because his participation in the band has ticked off a lot of people.  Wolfie is not Mikey and I'm glad. Mikey is missed, but I like Wolfie . The dude can play and I think he brings a fresh new outlook to the band.  I just hope he doesn't get ruined by the business.  In a sort of neener-neener moment, I want to say that I supported Wolfie from the beginning. I was bummed that Mike was gone, but the idea of three Van Halens playing Van Halen music really piqued my interest. I'm glad to say that Wolfie doesn't disappoint.<br /><br />We'll go with Dave next. Man, is it good to have him back. He just has a certain style that makes Eddie play a certain way. As far as partnerships go, Eddie and Dave are up there with Lennon/McCartney or Richards/Jagger. His lyrics have become more metaphysical and, dare I say, introspective. Still, his wit is intact and he doesn't shy away from the sublime or the ridiculous. No one can ride the line between clever and stupid like Dave, and the album works largely because Dave is back.<br /><br />Alex Van Halen has always been one of my favorite drummers and it is good to hear some new stuff from him. He turns in another excellent performance on this album. His drum tone is instantly recognizable and his rhythm and timing leads to some of the most unique riffing patterns any drummer has ever played.  I will spend many nights wearing headphones so I can specifically focus on Alex. I can't say enough about the guy.<br /><br />Eddie Van Halen has been my favorite guitar player since I first heard him nearly 30 years ago. I know there are players out there that have technical skills that surpass Eddie, but no one plays like Eddie when he's on fire - and he's on fire throughout the album.He sounds like he has been trying to figure out new ways to play again and that is refreshing. It's a horrible thing when an artist and craftsman like Eddie feels like they are just going through the motions.  Eddie is obviously having fun and it is good to hear him wail again.<br /><br />Overall the album rocks. Even the weakest songs aren't that bad. The band has taken a lot of flak for redoing old demos, but after listening to the album a several dozen times, I think those old demos helped the band get in touch with their roots. Some of the old stuff does sound a little dated, but it works for the most part.<br /><br />\\"Tattoo\\" - I like this song a lot better now that I know it isn't the best song on the album. The intro guitar line is reminiscent of \\"Jamie's Cryin'.\\" I guess it used to be called \\"Down in Flames,\\" which I You Tubed for comparison. I heard a couple of different bootlegs but the old version didn't impress me either. I think \\"Tattoo\\" works just as well as it is.  One of my favorite solos from Eddie. Not a bad tune.<br /><br />\\"She's the Woman\\" - Now, I did have a pretty good bootleg of this from the original demo. It was a cool song then and is a cool song now, even with different lyrics. It delivers that hard funk Van Halen is known for. The new bridge is cool and the arpeggio solo section takes the song on a quick ride before returning to the main groove. Cool song all around.<br /><br />\\"You and Your Blues\\" - The first brand new track on the album. It has a sort of tricked out \\"Ain't Talkin' 'Bout Love\\" sort of intro riff but then evolves into a solid mid-tempo rocker. This one's a sleeper and may take a while to get used to, though there is plenty of good playing on it. Cool tune.<br /><br />\\"China Town\\" - Another new one. Is a double-time rocker and one of the heaviest tunes on the album. The verse has a cool breakneck groove, and the chorus wails. Great solo by Eddie and Wolfie. A favorite.<br /><br />\\"Blood and Fire\\" - This is a very cool tune. Parts of it came from a movie in the 80s called \\"The Wild Life,\\" but it sounds like a brand new song on \\"Truth.\\" Eddie has a great solo wrapped up with some great riffs by Alex. Another favorite.<br /><br />\\"Bullethead\\"  - This is another oldie. I like its punk rock vibe. It's kind of obnoxious but has some good playing in it. Another sleeper.<br /><br />\\"As Is\\" - This is another crazy brand new Van Halen song. Alex kicks it off with one of his signature drum riffs as the guitar and bass lay down a heavy groove. Then the band kicks into high-octane boogie mode while Dave quick-talks through his trademark philosophical jive that makes perfect sense, yet no sense at all.  The outro fades under feedback and speed metal riffs. Freaking awesome.<br /><br />\\"Honeybabysweetiedoll\\" - A surprisingly heavy tune considering its title. This is no pop tune. Instead the band throws down some of the heaviest riffage of the album on this song. It is supposedly taken from an old song called \\"Act Like it Hurts,\\" but there's barely a resemblance. If Dave were spitting some bit about death and destruction in a cookie monster voice, this would easily be a circle pit classic. Cool tune, but probably not for the casual fan.<br /><br />\\"The Trouble With Never\\" - This is the first song from the album that I caught myself singing unconsciously. I didn't immediately like this song at all, but for some reason it's the one my brain latched on to and out of nowhere I would start singing the chorus. It has since become one of my favorites.  It's got a funky little breakdown that has Dave dropping verse like a beat poet over a wash of Eddie's guitar feedback. Cool tune<br /><br />\\"Outta Space\\" - This used to be called \\"Let's Get Rockin',\\" and from what I can tell it was a pretty rocking tune back then. The 2012 version is quite the rocker too, with the main musical parts staying pretty much intact. Dave's lyrics show a more evolved sense of songwriting. Good to hear the song as an official release.  Another good tune.<br /><br />\\"Stay Frosty\\" - Dave, in full used-car-salesman-turned-sidewalk-prophet mode, leads the band through a boogie-woogie blues for the new millennium.  Meanwhile the Van Halens keep the tune chooglin' along with a good, solid rock n' roll shuffle into a classic Eddie guitar solo wrapping up with a headrush of bass, drum and guitar licks. It's kind of a goofy tune but not bad overall.<br /><br />\\"Big River\\" - I also have an old demo of this song, which used to be called \\"Big Trouble.\\" The music is basically the same, but the lyrics have changed. Still a good song.  It's good to have some official version of this tune.<br /><br />\\"Beats Workin'\\" - The album closer is also a song from the band's early days called \\"Put Out the Lights.\\" The song works better in its modern form largely due to the new lyrics. Wolfie gets a short solo spot with some overdriven bass before Eddie's blazes through one of his signature solos. Overall, not a bad way to end an album.<br /><br />I give it three and a half stars because it is far from perfect. (I couldn't figure out how to make a half star.)<br /><br />The new songwriting style picks up where \\"Me Wise Magic\\" and Van Halen 3 left off - gone are the days of songs with only two parts. I like that as musicians they have evolved, but I would like to hear their take on a simple song. The production is modern and that means loud with some digital overdrive. It's not too distracting, but it's there. Every instrument and the vocals are in their place, but it doesn't sound too polished, for better or worse.<br /><br />Ultimately, \\"Truth\\" documents a band in transition. The album is like a mix of the band's earlier albums \\"Diver Down\\" with its half old/half new feel, and \\"Women and Children First\\" with its punky aggressiveness. Those albums were also transition albums of sorts and that makes me wonder how the next album will turn out, as \\"Fair Warning\\" and \\"1984\\" were strong follow ups, though \\"Warning\\" was a late bloomer.<br /><br />There you go, a lifelong fan's perspective, though VH could drop a turd in the middle of the table on Christmas Day and I would eat it up and find something good to say about it. So take it how you will, but rock on.

B007I1QUYE|5.0|I was a minor comic book geek as a kid (Marvel primarily, not DC), and I have to admit, I was fairly pleased when the Raimi-envisioned Spiderman series went into production.  The actual comic book was a decent mix of drama, action, and humor, and I was pleased that Sam Raimi did a good job of infusing a lot of those same elements into the movies.<br /><br />I bought the \\"Spiderman Trilogy Blu-ray\\" series just a few weeks ago, after having never purchased ANY of the single movies either on DVD or Bluray (which is actually surprising for me as I enjoyed them for the most part), so there has been a fair amount of time between the recent purchase and my last viewing of most of the series.  I decided to watch all three again over a week, and I have to admit I was pleasantly surprised.  I honestly didn't remember that the whole series was as good as it is.  Is it phenomenal?  No, but it is a solid, interesting, and entertaining presentation of the Peter Parker / Spiderman story.<br /><br />I'm not one of those nitpicks who lambasts every little detail that's different from the actual comic book.  I try to consider the bigger picture, the spirit of intent, so to say, and take the movie production as a whole.  For the most part, this trilogy hits the target.  Tobey Maguire delivers a nuanced but impacting Peter Parker, and Kirsten Dunst delivers the right balance of emotion and spunk as Mary Jane Watson.  The other supporting characters (the Green Goblin, Doc Ock, the Sandman, Venom, Aunt May, etc) deliver with variable impact, but all still carry their weight in the series.  The weaker of the three films is number 3 (as most reviewers, myself included, seem to think), but it's still good enough to ably support the series as a whole.<br /><br />Not sure I would give any of the individual movies a solid 5, but the series as a whole is, for me, a definite \\"4.5 with a roundup to 5\\" rating.<br /><br />Pluses would be the story and character development, with weighty-enough performances delivered by the various actors in the series (especially the mains) and the well-balanced delivery of light and heavy plot lines.  Even when the story gets serious, there's a comical element to the whole delivery.<br /><br />Minuses would be the \\"everything and the kitchen sink\\" approach to the third installment.  There's really just too many story elements that distract from a central theme to sink your teeth into.  It almost works...but not quite.<br /><br />Again, having never watched the entire series in quick succession, I was impressed by how well it held up as a whole.  If for some fluky reason, like me, you haven't purchased any of the previous single titles, this is way worth the buying the series a whole.  Though I somewhat enjoyed the newer 2012 re-imagining of Spiderman, I admit, this interpretation of the story offers something the newer delivery didn't.

1400067820|5.0|Taleb is candid about what to do if you have all the money in the world  -- in his parlance, f*** you money.  He does, and the suggestion would be to follow his example.<br /><br />More easily said than done.  Although he dwells at some length on the intelligence of his ancestors, he is quiet about his own.  I'm a smart enough guy that I don't often encounter people markedly more intelligence except through their writing.  Taleb is clearly one such - a five or six sigma fellow, to use the kind of traditional statisics which he despises.  The former is one mind in three million, the latter one in a billion.  That is, if you believe standard statistics, which would be blind to the existence of exceptional subpopulations such as Jews and Lebanese from Amouin, in other words, standard stats would ignore black swans and get it wrong.<br /><br />Just as it is easy to be to be charitable if you are wealthy, it is easy to be moral and ethical if you are blindingly intelligent.  It is equally easy to have contempt for the ordinary strivers.  Taleb disdains those who have come up via the national merit scholarship, valedictorian, Princeton, Harvard Law School etc. etc. path.  People who are far brighter than the average, yet not so bright that they have the freedom to paint outside the lines of their lives.  People who have a perpetual anxiety, and a need to get ahead of their fellow strivers, and certainly the hoi polloi, by whatever means.  Especially if it means adopting a convenient moral blindness.  He is not shy about naming such shysters by name: Thomas Friedman, Alan Blinder, Myron Scholes, Robert Merton.  It is a long list.<br /><br />Egregiously making enemies is one of the privileges given to those who possess f*** you money .  He proudly recounts the bullet embedded deeply in his father's chest, requiring him to carry X-rays to explain his failure to pass through airport metal detectors.  He talks about his own bodybuilding regime, giving him a sufficiently formidable aspect to discourage physical retribution for his verbal assaults.  He invites the reader to share his prejudices.  He's successful with me.  I can't be him, but I admire him.  I'm the kind of guy with enough experience in business, government and academia to instantly recognize the kinds of phony he detests, but without the intellect to articulate the revulsion nearly so well, or the cojones to beard them in their respective dens.<br /><br />Now we come to the title, \\"Antifragile.\\"  Something is fragile if it an event not too far out of the ordinary can damage it.  A teacup is fragile.  A banking system or a currency is fragile if it cannot withstand the sort of shocks that should be expected over the course of time.  Moreover, fragile things can generally only be worsened by life's slings and arrows.  No chance event is going to improve a teacup. .. it is all downside.<br /><br />A robust system reacts better to the twists and turns of life.  A plastic teacup is robust in the face of children's small accidents.  The Internet is built to be robust.  Composed of electronic components any of which may fail, it has enough redundancy that a message can almost always get through.  There are, of course, exceptions, which the (fragile) government security people use to scare us when they need funding or greater permission to invade our lives.<br /><br />Antifragility is more than robustness.  It is the property of improving under small shocks.  A system of 1000 small restaurants, each of them individually fragile - likely to be forced out of business quite easily - is quite robust.  Whatever happens to the economy, the traveler will always find one which remains open to feed him.  Each failure gives the others an opportunity to learn, to adapt, and to make themselves stronger.  The greatest antifragile system in the world is evolution.  Individuals of a species are fragile, the species itself is at least robust, and life itself is antifragile.  Even great shocks, such as the meteor strike which caused the Permian extinction, serve in the long run to improve it.<br /><br />Taleb relishes his background as a trader, an artisan, a practitioner, a guy who puts his money where his mouth is.  He uses trading as a metaphor.  Situations with a large upside but limited downside he calls \\"convex,\\" or antifragile.  Call options would generally fall in this category.  Conversely, those with limited upside but potentially unlimited downside, such as AIG's reinsurance of collateralized debt obligations and swaps, are fragile or \\"concave.\\"  They break, as AIG did break in the collapse of 2007/08.  The trader Taleb made tons of money betting against the fragile Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac.  The upside, if people paid their mortgages, was quite modest.  The downside if they could not was huge.<br /><br />Convex situations provide \\"optionality.\\"  Like a call option, they provide unlimited upside and limited downside risk.  Taleb's major rant is against the elites of our society who can only benefit from optionality.  Investment managers who lose millions in other people's money while drawing fat salaries, then when their funds go bust, as Corzine and Scholes' have twice, move on to other opportunities, leaving the little people to lick their wounds.  Journalists like Thomas Friedman whose beating of the war drums helped draw us into Iraq, but who suffered no loss of any kind when it turned out to be a disaster.  He hates hypocrites like Peter Orszag, who played a role in precipitating the meltdown of 2007, then without shame moved into the Obama administration to supposedly fix it.  Ditto Bernanke.<br /><br />He admires people who are not just \\"tawk,\\" who live the lives according to the principles they advocate, and put up with large amounts of heat in the process.  He names Ralph Nader, Steve Jobs and Andre Malraux among his heroes.<br /><br />How can we apply his lessons to our lives?  As far as money goes, he is a trader.  Trading is generally a zero-sum game.  His advice would be that of his character Fat Tony, \\"Don't be a sucker.\\"  Unfortunately, in just about every trade there is a sucker, somebody on the wrong side of it.  It his hard to find a moral course of action in such a situation.  The dumb guy loses.  He trusts his banker, his broker, and his lawyer.  It is the agency problem - these guys are not looking out for their clients, they are looking out for themselves.  Taleb is indignant, but that's how the world works.  Dumb people don't have much choice.  They cannot be their own banker, broker or lawyer.  A guy like Taleb can.  NB: Google \\"Four Prominent Bastards\\" for Ogden Nash's poem on same.  Wonderful humor, and nothing has changed in 60 years.<br /><br />Taleb therefore advises us how to find convex risks in our own investments.  He doesn't dwell as much on the topic of investments that are convex from the perspective of a whole society.  He talks about why Switzerland works - it is a confederation of cantons, manageable entities - and talks about the benefits of family-owned businesses.  His advice would be that the managers of a business should have \\"skin in the game.\\"<br /><br />Taleb develops his barbell theme from \\"The Black Swan.\\"  With regard to your portfolio, keep most of it in very safe investments (whatever those are in these times - real estate, gold and ammunition?) and make speculations with convex exposure (ie, call options) with a small fraction.  Investing most of a portfolio in \\"fairly safe\\" stuff like AAA rated bonds can turn badly against you.<br /><br />He talks about risks in terms of the Pareto 80:20 rule.  20% of the people have 80% of the money.  20% of those have 80% of that (ie, 4% and 64%).  Focus on those 20%, or 4%.  If they are positive, back up the truck.  If they are negative, short them or get rid of them.<br /><br />Iatrogenics (ya-trow-jen-iks) is doctor-induced disease.  Doctors have an agency problem: it is your body, not theirs.  They have a bias towards action: a doctor who tells you just to wait, it will go away, appears not to be giving useful advice.  They have a financial incentive: they get paid more if they treat you.  In our litigious society they have a CYA incentive.  It is easier to get sued for doing nothing than following the accepted course of treatment.  The upshot is that we get far more treatment than is good for us.  Taleb advises that you ask the question, what would the doctor do if he had the problem, not you?  Odds are strong it would be much less.<br /><br />Iatrogenics comes into play in most professional spheres.  A fund manager cannot be faulted for going into Apple.  Every other fund manager is doing it.  Bernanke cannot be faulted for endless Quantative Easing.  All the other central banks are doing it.  The agent, the paid professional manager, is interested in his next paycheck, not the long term performance of the fund.  He has little or no skin in the game.  So, to him, it is merely a game.  Be glib and facile talking to investors, and move on when the game is up.<br /><br />He lambastes the American system of education, overstuffed with government money, risk-averse, never impacted by the faults in the supposed knowledge it imparts to its victims (`scuse, pupils).  His own education was ideal.  His father let him read pretty much whatever he wanted, and he acquired a superb classical education.  The book is replete with apt quotations from both well known and obscure scholars from antiquity.  He says that everything worthwhile which he learned, he taught himself.  Much of it came while he was hiding out from the civil war in Lebanon.<br /><br />I will refer back to Taleb as I plan the education of my infant son.  One must make allowances; there is an outside chance my kid won't have a five-sigma intelligence.  Still, the principle of taking responsibility, of educating one's self, should apply to any child.  It used to be the standard back in Lincoln's day.  Allow a kid to progress as far as his talents will take him, develop a broad understanding of the world, and avoid the Harvard hothouse flower syndrome which pushes a kid to achieve 150% of that of which he is capable, and leaves him quaking in fear all his life lest the fraud be discovered.<br /><br />I'll revisit this review upon rereading the book, which I plan to do shortly.  It is incredibly rich.  Here I offer just a few scoops of frosting, without even looking back to the cake itself.a zero-sum game. 0553386697|4.0|The Whole Brain Child was written by Daniel J. Siegel and Tina Payne Bryson. Siegel received his medical degree from Harvard University and did his postgraduate education at UCLA, where he studied child, adolescent, and adult psychiatry. Bryson earned her Ph.D. from the University of Southern California, where her research included attachment science, childrearing theory, and interpersonal neurobiology. They share scientific information on children's brains and what makes them different from a fully developed brain. The book is helpful to those who interact with children and was very interesting for me. I enjoyed reading it because I received a lot of new knowledge about the development of the brain and how it reacts to certain emotional situations, especially the brains of children. I feel like it has improved my understanding of children and helped to better my interactions with them. The book is an easy read and presents a lot of detail through charts and diagrams of brain activity, which really helps illustrate their concepts and ideas.<br />The Whole Brain Child tells us that brain research continues to show that childhood is a necessary time of growth and that every single experience affects the brain. The strategies the authors present help us to understand the necessity to develop a strong connection between a child's logical and emotional parts of their brain. This assists them in managing and considering their feelings and behaviors, and to help us develop an understanding of them as well. The book presents 12 strategies to help children's brains develop effectively. The authors tell us that there are many different parts of the brain with their own function, but it is essential for these parts to integrate and work together. The first two strategies are about right and left brain integration. The left side of the brain controls logic, and the right side of our brain controls emotion.  The authors tell us we can help with this integration by connecting with children's emotions. When a child is upset, we should help them link their emotions to events to understand why they feel a certain way. The next three strategies are about integrating the upper and lower brain. The top of our brain lets us think about our actions, and the lower part of our brain is instinct and survival. By integrating these parts, we develop opinions and morals, and this will shape how we act in an instinctive situation. The next two strategies tell us that remembering how events affect us positively and negatively is important to children's brain growth. Instead of ignoring something that happened because we do not like how it made us feel, we need to remember that experience to help our brain learn from it and grow. The next three strategies tell us that children need to accept who they are to be able to make decisions that are best for them in their own life. The last two strategies are about how essential it is to create relationships with others to help children think about how their decisions affect not only them, but others around them.<br /> This book connects to what we are learning in our course because it is all about the brain. The book focuses mostly on mentalism because it suggests that our brain is responsible for our logical and emotional behavior. However, the book proposes that our brain can still partly be shaped by experiences in our life and that as we are growing up and developing, our brain can also go through some changes from outside influences. We learned in our course that each part of our brain has different specialties and functions, and that connects to what the book is saying about each part of our brain being responsible for different thoughts and behavior. All parts of our brain need to work together to keep our brain healthy.<br /> I really enjoyed reading this book. It gave me great insight on how to better understand children and how they deal with their emotions in different situations. It was especially useful to me because I work with kids. I am a dance teacher and a camp counselor for kids as young as five years old. Sometimes it can be very difficult to understand how they are feeling and how to deal with them when they are acting out. My first reaction is to yell at them and discipline them when they do something wrong. However, after reading this book, I now realize that this is not the best approach. Growing up is an important time for a child and everything for them is a learning experience. They do not exactly understand why they feel a certain way or why the do the things that they do. The book has taught me that this is because their right and left brain aren't working together yet. Their brains aren't fully developed yet to help them take a step back and think logically about a situation instead of just to instinctively react emotionally. When a child is upset and acting out, you cannot just yell at them. You need to connect with them emotionally before you can talk logically with them. If you right away ask, \\"Why did you do that?\\" or \\"What were you thinking?\\" they will not be able to give you a logical response. Their first reaction is to act on their emotions and to take their feelings to the extreme. Once you acknowledge their feelings, you can connect with them. Then you can ask them why they feel that way or why they did what they did. Once you respond with the emotional part of their brain, they will be more comfortable when you try to connect to the logical part of their brain. This book was a good read and was helpful to me especially since I work with children. It explained how children's brains work really well and even tried to be specific to different age groups. It was really easy to understand, even for someone who knows nothing about neuroscience or the brain. However, I felt like the book was more directed towards parents, and I read this book for a neuroscience class. I give this book 4 out of 5 stars.

B000N2HBSO|5.0|As is the case for many who have just posted reviews, I read this book to see what Spielberg used as his historical source for his movie \\"Lincoln.\\" I was surprised to see that the whole movie is based on the final two chapters of the book, and that much of the movie focuses on content and characters -- especially around the passage of the 13th Amendment -- about which the book does not go into great detail. At any rate, it is difficult to understand the motivations of the characters in the movie without the greater understanding that the book provides. So to get the full impact of the times, I think it's best to read the book and then to see the movie. Each makes the other more meaningful and enjoyable.<br /><br />As for the book and Doris Kearns Goodwin, who I have admired for years in her television appearances, \\"Team of Rivals\\" seems to be more than a history. It focuses on our own times and the need for working together to escape political catastrophes then and now. Ms. Goodwin spends the first half of the book establishing the political context of the time. She points out that Lincoln's generation felt cheated that there were not more important things for them to accomplish compared to what the Founding Fathers had done to establish the nation. But the Founding Fathers had failed to resolve the slavery question, and it was left to Lincoln and his cabinet to solve it -- an issue that would prove much more challenging.<br /><br />In my mind, Goodwin uses this example as a parable for our own time. Does the need to end slavery compare to our own struggles with the inequality of classes and the end of the American Dream? I'm not sure. I guess each generation feels it is caught in some upheaval that is worse than what others faced. She holds Lincoln's example up to us to lead us out of our troubles, which also seems to be the primary reason Spielberg produced his movie at this time. The message is for our current leaders to work together as Lincoln and his cabinet were successful in doing.<br /><br />The other thing that was interesting about the book was the way Ms. Goodwin worked through her topics by finding primary sources from important people at the time -- diaries, letters home, and other personal descriptions -- that helped her to write as though she was personally observing those historical events. In addition to simply reporting on the events she was able to analyze why and how Lincoln was able to find just the right solutions not just for the political and military events of the time but also the personal hopes and aspirations of his cabinet. The point is made over and over in the second half of the book how Lincoln's character and empathy enabled him to handle impossible situations with great skill. Using this approach, Ms. Goodwin paints Lincoln  as the greatest leader in our history.<br /><br />I read \\"Van Gogh: The Life\\" a year ago where the authors took a similar approach to psychoanalyze Van Gogh. Like Goodwin's book, the Van Gogh book is over 800 pages long. They explored Van Gogh's own daily letters to his brother plus other primary sources to look at every aspect of his life. When you get that close to anyone, you're bound to dig up a lot of dirt. In fact, that book was quite negative about Van Gogh, and many admirers of the painter were upset that the authors had trashed their hero.<br /><br />Which makes Lincoln's story all the more remarkable. Ms. Goodwin has done a tremendous amount of research here and what she has presented portrays a President who is even greater than we had all learned in our history classes. It is timely reading for our own generation when we need people to think beyond the politics and act like leaders.ere successful in doing.<br /><br />The other thing that was interesting about the book was the way Ms. Goodwin worked through her topics by finding primary sources from important people at the time -- diaries, letters home, and other personal descriptions -- that helped her to write as though she was personally observing those historical events. 0812984412|5.0|Cloud Atlas is one of the 10 greatest novels I've ever read (and I read a lot!) Deeply human, the novel explores our connection and the effects of disconnection. Subtly, David Mitchell holds a mirror to some of the deepest shadows of the current Western culture, projecting those shadows in the future for the reader to consider possible futures, desired and feared. But, this novel is no polemic; the reader will not find political rants. Our own politics, past, present, and perhaps future are woven into the weft with deep craft. It's clear to me why the Wachoskis wanted to make this into a movie. Read the book first - it stands monumentally on its own terms.<br /><br />The writing craft in this book is truly astounding. Each historical voice reflects the ideas of its time just as much as expounding a particular character's viewpoint and internal process. That alone would be astonishing; each of these voices is entirely unique; each rings entirely true both as distinct being, but also in it's particular culturally defined cognitive perception. But that's not all: each voice is written in the language of the time. Again, these all ring true. I have read a great deal of mid-19th century literature, as well as 1930s, 70s. Mitchell nails these distinct views so that one is brought into these historical periods completely. That's the setup to reveal the truth from the future he outlines, as frightening and abhorrent as that future is.<br /><br />If you are committed to the current conflation of corporate economy and representative state, Cloud Atlas may very well rock your world.

B000FC1HBY|5.0|As in the first book the style remains the same. You don't read chapters of a saga but rather chapters of what is happening to various characters in the book. And the number of characters continue to grow! But isn't real life made of lots of people interacting and creating situations that affect others? My impression is that the books are written in a \\"TV Soap\\" style. We see things happening to various characters in their day to day challenges. Like in real life, the good don't necessarily succeed. Some characters you get really wrapped up in. My favorites are Tyrion, Jon, Daenerys and Arya. When I hit one of those chapters it is hard to put the book down. If you are looking for a more focused plot, you will be disappointed. You do get a sense that the plot will evenutally revole around the conflict between the enemy north of the wall, Daenerys and her dragons and the people we are currently reading about south of the wall. But for now it is a story about different individuals and how they are coping with the various pressures created by the triangle of conflict. This is not a bad thing. You sympathize for the characters in their decisions to try to deal with their situations not knowing all the details. It reads like real life situations. As the first the book is very long, almost three times a regular book. What is great, the author does not repeat or re-explain details to fill the book. It also means you really need to keep track of the characters. The book is well worth the money. Looking forward to start book 3 !

B000B5OD4I|5.0|Overall, this is a really nice watch.  It doesn't quite have the panache of vintage Seiko 6105 and 6309 cushion-cased dive watches, but it has a considerable following in the dive watch community, and is a favorite for customizing.  Numerous bands, dials, hands, and bezels are available so you can make your watch uniquely your own.  Also, you can wear this watch while you search for your vintage Seiko, if you're so inclined.  The only problems I found were that the lugs for the watch band showed visible tool marks, and the holes for the spring bars were a little crude, not the precisely drilled holes of my Tag-Heuer or vintage Seiko.<br />  On the plus side, while Seiko kinetic dive watches are not generally known for their accuracy, I must have gotten a good one.  My watch lost only 27 seconds over the first 10 days of use.  Having apparently settled in, it lost only an additional 6 seconds over the next 10 days.  While not quartz-level accuracy, this is outstanding for a mechanical watch and truly exceptional for a Seiko automatic dive watch.<br />  The metal Jubilee bracelet, while not great, is not as bad as many people seem to think.  The outer 2 and center links are solid metal, while the remaining 2 inner links and end pieces are formed sheet metal.  It's functional, very flexible, and comfortable.  If you feel like replacing it you can, but it's not an absolute necessity.<br />  My only real complaint is regarding the warranty.  If your order is fulfilled by Amazon, your watch will not be covered by the Seiko warranty because Amazon is not an authorized Seiko dealer.  Amazon will remove the warranty information, warranty registration card, list of authorized Seiko repair shops, and watch instructions from the watch box because, according to Amazon, they are \\"void.\\"  Why Amazon considers the Seiko instruction manual to be void because the Seiko warranty doesn't apply is not clear to me.  The generic instructions Amazon supplies with the watch are neither accurate nor complete.  However, the appropriate instruction manual may be downloaded from the Seiko website.<br />  Instead, Amazon offers its own 2-year limited warranty covering only the watch movement.  According to the express terms of the warranty, it does not cover problems with the bezel, crown, crystal, band, or case.  In other word, there are many, many things which could go wrong with your watch which would not be covered.  I'm not sure whether or not the dial and hands would be considered part of the watch movement, and therefore covered.  I haven't had any problems with my watch yet and haven't attempted to use the warranty.  It may turn out to be outstanding. However, my repeated attempts to register my watch online for the Amazon warranty were unsuccessful, and my e-mails to Amazon requesting assistance remain unanswered, so I'm not optimistic.rate nor complete. 0345536886|4.0|John Grisham is a best-selling machine in contemporary literarure. He is to legal drama what Tom Clancy is to military intrigue and what Ludlum was to the espionage thriller and what J.K. Rowling is to YA fantasy. And, as it turns out, Grisham could write funny things. THE LITIGATORS introduces a cast of characters so disreputable even Deck Shifflet crosses the street when they approach.<br /><br />One of Grisham's recurring themes surfaces, the one with the idealistic lawyer going toe-to-toe with a giant corporation. THE LITIGATORS tracks the misadventures of Finley & Figg, a two-man \\"boutique\\" law firm nestled in one of Chicago's low-rent districts. Oscar Finley is the crusty senior partner (he hates his wife). Wally Figg is the rotund junior partner (and a recovering alcoholic). Both are ham-and-eggers who rely on relentless ambulance chasing for their bread and butter. Neither had ever tried a case in court. They prefer to settle. Not everyone can be Perry Mason or Atticus Finch. Wally Figg, the more ambitious of the two, dreams of seeing his mug planted on bus stop billboards and in television adverts.<br /><br />Business is rough, cases are few and rarely lucrative. The last thing the firm and its shady operators needed was to hire young Harvard Law graduate David Zinc. David had, earlier today, suffered a nervous breakdown, quit his fast-track career at a prestigious law firm, and had gone on a drinking binge. David Zinc desperately requires a change of pace. He mourns the past five years he'd wasted as a nameless cog in a vast machinery. And he, too, had never tried a case in court. Finley and Figg take him in, anyway. At this stage, you could drop a Three Stooges reference and it wouldn't be inappropriate.<br /><br />I mentioned that Wally Figg is the ambitious one? He's always had a jones for the easy buck, for castles in the air. His latest scheme involves jumping in bed with all them other lawyers gearing up in a mass tort case against Varrick Labs, a Big Pharma company responsible for producing a drug called Krayoxx which reduces cholesterol but also may be inducing heart attacks. Wally is salivating, with an eye towards a sure thing, the easiest class action suit ever. He drags a reluctant Oscar and David along. He signs up clients who have either taken Krayoxx or had lost someone who'd taken Krayoxx. He files his motions. And then the \\"boutique\\" law firm of Finley & Figg sits back and waits for this pharmaceutical giant to crumble and pay up. A cocky Wally even signs notes promising his clients millions of settlement money.<br /><br />So, yeah, overnight, the Finley & Figg law firm became specialists in mass tort litigation. But they should've brushed up on their Perry Mason and their Atticus Finch.<br /><br />For whatever reason I hadn't picked up a Grisham novel in years. THE LITIGATORS returns me to the fold, even if it's not as suspenseful or electric or, frankly, as ambitious as, say, THE FIRM or THE RUNAWAY JURY. It's a lightweight read, but I couldn't stop reading. It's a sly, tongue-in-cheek expos&eacute; on the seedier elements of lawyering. I like the lead character. David Zinc makes for a likable protagonist, likable because of his inexperience and his idealism and his resolve (although, okay, in some ways, he's too passive). He somehow reminds me of Mitch McDeere and Rudy Baylor. David's presence serves as a grounding element whereas Finley & Figg tend to come across as caricatures who endlessly bicker and unscrupulously practice law. However, Finley & Figg, with their bottom-feeder escapades, also provide chuckles.g Pharma company responsible for producing a drug called Krayoxx which reduces cholesterol but also may be inducing heart attacks. B004OT2WKO|5.0|My Review for people new to Technic building...<br /><br />THE GREAT<br /><br />01: As with other Lego Technic kits, the mechanical design of the Unimog is a thing of beauty. Watching the progress develop from a seemingly disconnected collection of complex mechanical components into a full, working truck (with arm) is really profound fun. Well, if your a geek like me. Also, the fact that this model is of the UNIMOG, which is the most unsexy hunk of truck on this earth, makes it even more attractive. The \\"utility\\" of this model shines though, especially when watching all the working pistons and gears moving.<br /><br />02. The instructions, while at times a tad obscure, are also a thing of wonder. Imagine being a drafter and being told to demonstrate how to build a multi differential, wildly complex machine WITHOUT USING WORDS. I say ANY company, who demands that their customers build their products, should take a long hard look at how Lego solves these problems with well rendered graphics.<br /><br />03: Sturdy! It doesn't fall to bits when rolling it around.  While I wouldn't let my 5 year old at it..this thing can take some abuse. But really, this model should be sitting on a \\"shelf of pride\\". It's not meant for sand box thrashing. But that's just me.<br /><br />04: Forgiving! I adore that Lego let's you make mistakes. They design for your ineptitude! How rare is that? For once I'm not being subjected to engineers that think I'm as smart as they are!<br /><br />UMMM...NOT SO GREAT<br /><br />01. Really Lego? I was missing THREE pieces? Any Lego builder knows that sinking feeling when your build comes to a stuttering stop because Lego didn't include every part. Yes, they have a great ordering section for missing parts. Yes, they have a great customer service, and I expect this happens allot. But this still is very un-fun. I hope Lego looks into this issue seriously, as I'm seeing this compliant more and more.<br /><br />ONLY TIPS THAT MATTER.<br /><br />01: Separate every part (per numbered bags) into bins! Organize!! You purchased this thing to BUILD it, not to play some mind numbing hide n seek game with parts that look alike. Do the work of organizing and you will have improved the build experience by 90%. I let my 5 year old organize all the colors and parts, and she loved it!<br /><br />02: Slow down! The build is the fun part!! Take your time and really follow those directions. Unbuilding, because you miscounted holes five steps ago, is really not so much fun.<br /><br />FINAL<br /><br />The UNIMOG is an excellent example of just how far Lego have come, and really shows off some tremendous engineering/design talents. I maintain this is more than a toy, but a work of industrial design. I really loved the process of building the UNIMOG, I think more than any Lego set, and while I was very disappointed with the discovery of some missing pieces, I'm still more than satisfied. This set will delight those of you who love watching the under workings of complex mechanical systems and is an educational experience by default.<br /><br />Lego really nailed this one.<br /><br />Now I have to justify the $400 bucks for Technic Off-Road.....Gulp. Heck, you only live once!

1451627297|5.0|With this book Stephen King impressed me and not only because I already was a great fan of his books before (even if I absolutely do not agree with his politics). \\"11.22.63\\" is one of the best, most intelligent and most profound time travel stories ever.<br /><br />As it is pretty obvious by just looking at the cover, the story turns around the assasination of John F. Kennedy and an attempt by a person from our times to prevent it. The man who finds the opportunity to travel back in time and try to save Kennedy's life is an altruist idealist, who hopes that he can change the history and as a result make our world a better place. He can not go directly to 1963 - he can only travel to 1958 and so has to live five years in the past before he can act. He knows that the risks are very high, because the past has ways to protect itself from the changes - and the more important the change is, the more lethal defenses are used... But still, he goes for it.<br /><br />The description of time traveller's life in the 50s is excellent. King took great care to study the details of all days life in this time and I really felt like I was in this time and place with the main character. I was also delighted to learn, that even if Stephen King is a very left-winged writer, his sexual fantasies are very conservative, in fact they seem similar to those of deeply catholic Sicilian peasants from the beginning of XX century...))) (remembering Mario Puzo's \\"Godfather\\" can help to understand the last sentence).<br /><br />The tension rises in this book all the time, surprises are well preserved and twists are served at just the right time. There is also a long and excellent sequence reminding the last chapters from \\"The day of the Jackal\\". Supernatural elements are very few as they are used to spice all the thing without drowning it in mumbo jumbo. But those things are pretty normal for King's books, which are almost always excellent - and therefore they are not the reason why I was so impressed by \\"11.22.63\\".<br /><br />I was impressed by this book for two reasons. First, Stephen King decided in his description of events in Dallas on 22 November 1963 to go against the opinion of a majority of US citizens (including his own wife, as he recognizes it in the - very interesting - postface). This disply of common sense and intelligence took some courage.<br /><br />The second reason was the conception of the time and of the mechanisms by which it defends itself against changes and of ways in which it can be changed, and of their various consequences. I can not go here into many details because I absolutely do not want to provide spoilers, but I was surprised and very impressed by the final chapters of this book. Considering once more how much left to the center King situates himself, writing the last chapters of this book asked for a LOT of courage. Also, settling the old argument between Leibniz and Voltaire in the way this book does, required a kind of wisdom rarely seen amongst self-declared \\"progressists\\".<br /><br />Bottom line, this is not only an excellent read but also one of the best, most powerful and most profoundly wise books by this very talented author, who with this novel just reached a whole new level of greatness. Enjoy!ssed by \\"11.22.63\\".<br /><br />I was impressed by this book for two reasons. 0547884591|5.0|The nicest thing about this book is the way the recipes are organized, especially in the main \\"Domestic\\" chapter. Whereas most cookbooks will divide recipes into \\"appetizers,\\" \\"poultry,\\" \\"dessert,\\" etc., Tim Ferriss divides his by increasing order of complexity. The first few lessons are super-easy and almost foolproof, and he builds on what you've already learned as you move forward. In the later chapters, things can get kind of complex, but the Domestic chapter is all about getting you to be comfortable cooking without too much stress.<br /><br />If I could make one change, it would be to note some of the adaptions that could be used. Ferriss talks a lot about grapeseed oil as his go-to oil, whereas many people probably have olive oil or canola oil sitting around in their pantries. It would be nice to know if these could work instead. Ditto for a stainless steel or non-stick pot, when he often recommends a combination cast iron dutch oven/skillet. Still, despite these few hang-ups, I consider this one of the best \\"learning to cook\\" books I've seen so far.

B00006CXSS|5.0|Band of Brothers is a 10-part account of the wartime experience of Easy Company, in the 506th Regiment of the 101st Airborne Division.  It begins with their training experience in Georgia and follows them to England, to D-Day in Normandy, Operation Market Garden in Holland, the siege of Bastogne and, eventually, the capturing of Hitler's Eagle Nest headquarters/estate in Bavaria.<br /><br />Featuring Damian Lewis, Ron Livingston, David Schwimmer, Donnie Wahlberg, et al. the miniseries (based on Stephen Ambrose's book) features the individual experiences of individual characters in separate episodes and contains opening commentary by the characters' real-life counterparts.  A sixth disc, containing material on the making of the miniseries, etc. features more interviews with the actual, surviving members of Easy Company.<br /><br />Under the leadership of Tom Hanks and Steven Spielberg, the miniseries contains a great deal of realistic footage of the sort seen in Saving Private Ryan.  Limbs are severed and the sounds of bullets and shrapnel whizzing through the air are omnipresent.  We feel the cold of Bastogne, luxuriate in the beauties of Austria and suffer through the training regimens to which the company is subjected.  Watching is not always easy or pleasant, but the emotional impact of the separate episodes is strong.<br /><br />The individual episodes are quite uneven.  Some are extremely powerful, e.g., the training episode and the D-Day episode (with stunning use of CGI).  Others are less satisfactory.  The Bastogne episode, for example, is a long, cold sojourn in foxholes with German artillery and mortar fire shattering trees and shattering bodies.  Civilians, however, will obtain a realistic sense of the actual nature of war.<br /><br />The production is courageous in representing the courage of some individuals, the incompetence of others, given the fact that the `characters' represent real people.  Given the fact that the narrative is historically accurate, our `wishes' as viewers sometimes go unsatisfied.  We want to see much more of Damian Lewis in the role of Dick Winters, for example, but the fact is that Dick Winters was promoted to a battalion position and could not be there, leading Easy Company, for the duration of the miniseries.<br /><br />In general, this is a superb miniseries and the subsidiary material on the sixth disc is excellent.  The actual, historical members of Easy Company speak for themselves with great but simple eloquence and their eyes tell a story of war that brings the reality home in ways that films can never quite do.e, our `wishes' as viewers sometimes go unsatisfied. B008V0OLOW|3.0|All right, so I'm using hyperbole for effect. This is clearly Diana Krall. But the style? Well honey, this vibe just doesn't fit you nearly as well as that Victoria Secret stuff you're wearing on the cover.<br /><br />On the plus side, this is a welcome change from Quiet Nights, her bossa nova experiment where Diana sounded not just relaxed. More like sleepwalking, most of the time. On Glad Rag Doll, Diana sounds wide awake, but out of place. She just doesn't do honky tonk, barrel house, or country-flavored tunes that well.<br /><br />The piano is subdued on most tracks, which is a disappointment because I believe piano playing is Diana's greatest asset. And I can recall instances of a cheap, tinny-sounding piano where her normal full grand piano would have been more welcome. At the end of the Deluxe edition, there are three quiet tracks where Diana sings and plays a better-sounding instrument, and these tracks might be the highlight of the album.<br /><br />The guitar sound is for the most part rather unpleasant. A ragged, raspy steely sound on some tracks, and a growling distorted electric guitar sound on others. Guitar seems to be more dominant than piano on this record, leaving Diana's singing as the main focus. This she performs competently, but she either does not have the voice or the sensibility to carry these songs off. The delivery is polite when it needs to be raunchy, in order to fit with the song and the rest of the instrumentation. That's my take on it at least. And I don't think Diana can pull off raunchy.<br /><br />On some level I appreciate and commend Krall on the urge to try out a different genre and a different sort of sound, a different sort of band. But the fact is that some artists are more versatile than others. Norah Jones for example sounds at home singing mock punk rock, country and western, blues, or straight jazz. Still, there are songs on this album Diana Krall could successfully add to her repertoire. Glad Rag Doll is the title trackfor a reason. It's one of the best performances here. All in all, I can only get into about a third of the songs on the Deluxe edition. A lot less on the standard release.<br /><br />For my money, if I was looking for an album of Diana doing something a little different, I would go for Paul McCartney's Kisses On The Bottom, where Diana is the main keyboard player in the backing band. She really shines there. I think she truly holds that album together.<br /><br />Next time I think we could hope for more of Krall's standard jazz piano playing, and something closer to the Great American Songbook. Diana, if you want to mix things up, then try adding a sax and a cornet to your band, not these raspy cheap-sounding guitars. Or vibraharp. Vibraharp would suit you nicely.

By the end of the book Doidge sounds like a broken record constantly repeating himself and stressing his thesis. This books is great if you're studying or interested in neuroscience and parts of psychology but it is also tedious which requires effort to finish in one sitting.

In a way, this disappoints me as I was hoping to hear it in the new order.  Infinte Sadness and Tonite (Reprise) are not included.<br /><br />This slipcase is absolutely gorgeous!  The artwork featured on the case is the artwork shown in the product thumbnail.  The surface is a nice gloss, with the art itself being raised slightly to give it a texture.  It is very well made, and each record has it's own sleeve.  On one side of the sleeve is a pattern of the MCIS dingbats and song listing, the other side has a large version of some of the original MCIS booklet artwork.  Newly packaged, each vinyl comes packed in a lined paper sleeve.  Additionally packaged are smaller sleeves within the sleeves, all of which have a monotone pattern akin to the original MCIS artwork.  The assumption is you can remove the paper sleeves and house them in the rigid cardboard sleeves that have the artwork.  (See image)<br /><br />There are two books that accompany the music.  One is basically a large version of the MCIS lyric sheet from the CD version.  However, it is in a different typeface.  A minor change.  The woodcut artwork remains the same.  The second booklet is an all new booklet that features a small recent article written by David Wild in which Billy talks about MCIS and the significance it had on him and the band, and where it lies as far as the \\"best time\\" in the history of Smashing Pumpkins.  Also in this booklet are notes by Billy on each song and how it evolved, the meaning, what he thinks of it, which is incredible insight much of which has not been revealed until now! Though it seems as if the booklety was designed fore the CD version, as the songs are divided between the classic \\"Dawn to Dusk\\" and \\"Twilight to Starlight\\"  There are also concept sketches for much of the MCIS artwork, done by Billy.  Much of this eventually evolved into the John Craig collages that MCIS is well known for.  The only thing that drives me crazy about this book is that they include some stills from some of the music videos (usually in the columns) but they are shown letterboxed (black bars on each side) WHY would the person doing the layout let this happen?  I understand that the change to widescreen changes the aspect ratio, but black bars could have very easily been removed and would make the appearance of these stills more appealing.  However. Hardly a distraction while listening to, and goes largely unnoticed.  I listened to these records in their entirety and I feel as though the recording is of a good quality.  It sounds the way vinyl should sound. B008FPZQRO|5.0|For many years Emerson, Lake, and Palmers \\"Tarkus\\", their second studio lp, was and still is one of my favorite progressive/classic rock albums of all time. Released in 1971 after the release of the hugely successful first lp, Tarkus was and is still ahead of it's time. Releasing a side long suite of the title track was unheard of in 1971, but ELP pulled it off. ELP hardly ever was this aggressive and ferocious in their musicianship. Keith Emerson and Carl Palmer play so wonderfully together, the layers of keyboards and drums, just fit together and with such precision, this track has to be heard to be believed. And of course Greg Lake vocals and beautiful guitar playing, just round everything out. And now the first two ELP lp's have been released in Deluxe Editions, and you can't ask for more. I picked up both, and was very pleased with how they have both been handled. Each edition carries 3 discs, 2 compact discs, and a 3rd Dvd audio disc. Disc 1 contains the original remastered lp. The second disc contains, brand new 2012 stereo altenate mixes of the songs, plus some unreleased tracks. Disc 3 is where most people will want to look into. It contains brand new high bitrate Advanced Resolution 5.1 surround sound mixes of the lp's, and also high resolution stereo mixes of songs taken directly from the original master tapes. Unfortunately some of the original masters for the first lp have either dissapeared or are missing, so one or two of the 5.1 mixes of songs (Tank with drum solo, and most of The Three Fates) from the first lp are not included. Also the ending of \\"Knife Edge\\" is different due to mixing problems. These tracks are substituted with other tracks to fill out the lp as close as possible to the original. It almost works but not perfect. You can listen to the whole complete lp in remastered stereo tho, and it still sounds wonderful. As far as \\"Tarkus\\" goes, the whole complete lp is mixed into beautiful Advanced Resolution (which is the highest quality) 5.1 surround sound and if any lp should be heard in surround, this is it. Originally recorded in 16 track stereo, the sound now opens and expands like originally intended. The album is clean, with nice bass response, spacious rear activity, and much more detail than ever before. Once again, the keyboards, and drums and percussion have to be heard to be believed. You will never hear \\"Tarkus\\" sound better. It is a beautiful listening experience. You must have a Dvd audio player with correct analog audio connecting cables to hear it uncompressed in Advanced Resolution Surround or Stereo, although you can also hear it in excellent DTS 5.1 (no Dolby Digital) and High Resolution Stereo on regular dvd players (with standard optical cable hookup). You also get nice reproductive lp covers, photos, liner notes explaining the history of the lp's. So although slightly dissapointed with the first lp (although it is understandable) I am extremely pleased with the 2nd lp, so all in all, I highly recommend both of these Deluxe Editions for either the die hard ELP fans or even new listeners who would like to get a true introduction to just how great a band ELP were and still are. I am sure the rest of ELP's original lp's will be released in same Deluxe Editions in the not so distant future. This was a group whose recorded output should be heard in the highest format possible.surround sound and if any lp should be heard in surround, this is it. B0090SI42Y|3.0|The Man With the Iron Fists is a movie that was 'presented' (not produced) by Quentin Tarantino and directed by RZA. It centers on the story of a black blacksmith in China, who is trying to leave his hostile home village that's entrenched in between good ol' clan wars.<br /><br />If you are seeking to be entertained, look no further. RZA blends the 'hip-hop samurai' type of music and feel -- prevalent in a lot of anime in recent years -- with the Crouching Tiger, Hidden Dragon-floating-around-style of fighting to create a high-flying (no pun intended) and adventurous parody of the genres with a Spaguetti Western flare. Needless to say, visually this movie is great in many respects.<br /><br />However, beyond that, the movie suffers from more than a few noticeable stumbling points. The first being dialogue. Not only is the writing over-simplified, but the fact that some of the Chinese characters have Oriental-English accents while others speak perfectly clear English is something that will distract some viewers from the already simple plot. In addition, the main antagonist Silver Lion is portrayed as the funny one-liner villain, but there are times when his role feels overdone and out of place during the more serious scenes. Perhaps this harkens back to the older Kung-fu movie time other reviewers mentioned, but for me it was distracting.<br /><br />Another caveat is the characters. They didn't feel very memorable, which is again probably due to the dialogue. The exception here is Russel Crowe's character, who exhibits wit, style, and the fact he sticks out like a sore thumb in a charming way. While I feel he did an extraordinary job in executing this role, I'm not exactly sure how his career will be affected in the long-run from this... You'll know what I mean.<br /><br />Lastly, there were gaps in terms of the suspension of belief. While these types of movies are going to exhibit combat and visuals that are uncanny, a few of the developments toward the end, including one bad guy in particular, just felt like huge leaps of faith without any sort of explanation at all. I don't want to spoil anything, but again I think you'll know what I mean as you watch.<br /><br />Now before I sound overly critical, let me tip my hat off to RZA. Not only did he direct the movie. He wrote it, helped compose the soundtrack, and starred as the blacksmith. This truly is RZA's movie in every sense of the phrase.<br /><br />With so many responsibilities on one person, I can't help but critique this movie with admiration for it. It may not be on the Tarantino level, but if this is a beginning path for RZA I am excited to see what he comes up with next. Watch TMWTIF with the right expectations. If you're expecting Kill Bill or Pulp Fiction, take it down a good notch. If you're expecting a parody of exaggerated, oriental combat and hip-hop anime with Spaghuetti Western tropes, you're in for an entertaining ride. It's worth a watch, even if it doesn't etch itself into your soul.

B0043GN6XK|5.0|I'm no opera buff, and I suppose Baroque opera is one corner of the field that I have deliberately shied away from, well, the beginning. A while back, however, I happened to listen to Vivaldi's Bajazet and was immediately engaged. That led me to try this recording of Ercole, and I found myself not only engaged but entrapped as well. Everything comes together in this two-disc set -- the composition itself, the orchestra, chorus and soloists -- to form an almost perfect sonic storm of delight. Most of us laymen know Vivaldi only by his concertos; this album testifies to his more encompassing talent in other forms as well. A magnificent release, highly recommended.
