B000001GXE|3.0|I have a different response to each of the performances on this disc. Other reviewers, one in particular, have analyzed these two Schubert renditions in far more detail and with much more musical understanding than I could ever aspire for, so let me contribute as a token layman, with accompanying ignorance. Kleiber's performance of the Symphony No. 3 I found perfectly acceptable. I'll agree that portions of it whiz by rather fast, but my attitude in such things is to wait and see if the difference can eventually be made to work or not. In this instance, I can go along with the quick paces -- it's different, but to me not especially unacceptable. Still, I will mention that I prefer to this performance of the No. 3 the versions conducted by Bruggen, Immerseel and Goodman -- all of which are by coincidence period instrument renditions. As for the recording of the Unfinished Symphony on this disc, I found it somewhat one-sided. The first movement certainly cast long dark shadows, rather in keeping with Kleiber's style on this disc, but the second movement seemed rather awkward, as if the conductor could not properly swivel his emotions to match the different outlook of the closing section. If we're talking about an alternate suggestion here, my choice is the vintage Klemperer account on EMI. Overall, I'd say these performances are okay -- if I could, I'd give the release 3.5 stars, but after some thought I've decided to aim on the low side on account of that second movement of the No. 8.

0064471837|4.0|\\"The worldbuilding in this book is absolutely amazing.\\"<br /><br />So said my friend and reading companion, and I find that I have to agree. Sabriel is a book that strikes one speechless with the sheer scope of it, the sheer uniqueness that it embodies. No more standard fantasy-fare. Oh, it does adhere to a quest-type structure; it follows the story of a girl on a journey - first to find her father, and then to prevent the unleashing of a great evil. But here is where the book utterly trashes conventionality.<br /><br />We go back to the worldbuilding. Garth Nix has a talent for this, for the setting of this world is unusual, to say the least. The Old Kingdom is a world full of magical peril, full of Dead beings that won't stay dead, and connected to a world of technology through a \\"Wall\\". Of course this causes problems. The heroine Sabriel finds herself the inheritor of an ancient legacy - as the Abhorsen, her task is to keep down the Dead, and to make sure they pass to the afterlife.<br />Of course, there are walking Dead aplenty. It makes for some very interesting scenes.<br /><br />The heroine herself is a conundrum. The word best used to describe her is \\"sassy\\", and yet she is much more than that, more than the sum of her parts. She is courageous, strong-willed and stubborn, but throughout the book one can see through that shell to her naivety, her weakness - she arrives in the Old Kingdom thinking she knows everything, but instead realizes the true limits of her knowledge, and the dangers all about her. The Old Kingdom is not tame. This fragility is what truly characterizes her, and makes her more than just words on a page. There are others of note, as well (such as Mogget, the adorably snarky cat!thing, and Touchstone, well...) but Sabriel stands out to me, especially, as powerful and well-rendered as she is.<br /><br />This book starts slow, but picks up speed a little way in, and soon the reader is drawn relentlessly into the story - and what a story it is! Full of darkness and horror, a sense of danger that cuts to the quick, even romance that subtly springs where the reader least expects it - it is a masterpiece in itself, moved by the machinations of such well-rendered characters. I will admit that it does lag in places, but as a whole Sabriel is well-written, and very well crafted.<br />As for myself... I have read quite a lot of books, yet Sabriel and its sequels remain at the tip of the iceberg, and nothing can seem to knock them from the pedestal. I've been in love with this series for approximately six years, and whatever happens, it will still inhabit its own little niche in my heart.<br /><br />\\"Does the walker choose the path, or the path the walker?\\"<br /> Full of darkness and horror, a sense of danger that cuts to the quick, even romance that subtly springs where the reader least expects it - it is a masterpiece in itself, moved by the machinations of such well-rendered characters.
